### Temples


Main articles: [Hindu temples in Varanasi](/wiki/Hindu_temples_in_Varanasi "Hindu temples in Varanasi") and [Religion in Varanasi](/wiki/Religion_in_Varanasi "Religion in Varanasi")
* [![The Kashi Vishwanath Temple, the most important temple in Varanasi.](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Benares-_The_Golden_Temple%2C_India%2C_ca._1915_%28IMP-CSCNWW33-OS14-66%29.jpg/188px-Benares-_The_Golden_Temple%2C_India%2C_ca._1915_%28IMP-CSCNWW33-OS14-66%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Benares-_The_Golden_Temple,_India,_ca._1915_(IMP-CSCNWW33-OS14-66).jpg "The Kashi Vishwanath Temple, the most important temple in Varanasi.")

The [Kashi Vishwanath Temple](/wiki/Kashi_Vishwanath_Temple "Kashi Vishwanath Temple"), the most important temple in Varanasi.
* [![Shri Vishwanath Mandir has the tallest temple tower in the world.[156]](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg/337px-New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg)](/wiki/File:New_Vishwanath_Temple_at_BHU.jpg "Shri Vishwanath Mandir has the tallest temple tower in the world.[156]")

[Shri Vishwanath Mandir](/wiki/Shri_Vishwanath_Mandir "Shri Vishwanath Mandir") has the tallest temple tower in the world.[[156]](#cite_note-Brief_description-158)
* [![The 18th century Durga Kund Temple](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Varanasi_India.jpg/300px-Varanasi_India.jpg)](/wiki/File:Varanasi_India.jpg "The 18th century Durga Kund Temple")

The 18th century [Durga Kund Temple](/wiki/Durga_Kund_Temple "Durga Kund Temple")
* [![Parshvanath Jain temple](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Jain_Mandir.JPG/326px-Jain_Mandir.JPG)](/wiki/File:Jain_Mandir.JPG "Parshvanath Jain temple")

[Parshvanath Jain temple](/wiki/Parshvanath_Jain_temple,_Varanasi "Parshvanath Jain temple, Varanasi")


Among the estimated 23,000 temples in Varanasi,[[36]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBansal200848–49-38) the temples most popular for worship are: the Kashi Vishwanath Temple of Shiva; the [Sankat Mochan Hanuman Temple](/wiki/Sankat_Mochan_Hanuman_Temple "Sankat Mochan Hanuman Temple"); and the Durga Temple, known for monkeys that reside in the large trees nearby.[[65]](#cite_note-Varun-67)[[157]](#cite_note-temple-159)[[30]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECunninghamSastri2002131–140-32)



* **The [Kashi Vishwanath Temple](/wiki/Kashi_Vishwanath_Temple "Kashi Vishwanath Temple")**, on the Ganges, is one of the 12 *[Jyotirlinga](/wiki/Jyotirlinga "Jyotirlinga")* Shiva temples in Varanasi.[[157]](#cite_note-temple-159) The temple has been destroyed and rebuilt several times throughout its existence. The [Gyanvapi Mosque](/wiki/Gyanvapi_Mosque "Gyanvapi Mosque"), which is adjacent to the temple, is the original site of the temple.[[158]](#cite_note-Warrier2014-160) The temple, which is also known as the Golden Temple,[[159]](#cite_note-route-161) was built in 1780 by Queen [Ahilyabai Holkar](/wiki/Ahilyabai_Holkar "Ahilyabai Holkar") of [Indore](/wiki/Indore "Indore"). The two [pinnacles](/wiki/Shikara "Shikara") of the temple are covered in gold and were donated in 1839 by [Ranjit Singh](/wiki/Ranjit_Singh "Ranjit Singh"), the ruler of [Punjab](/wiki/Punjab_(region) "Punjab (region)"). The dome is scheduled to receive gold plating through a proposed initiative of the Ministry of Culture and Religious Affairs of Uttar Pradesh. Numerous rituals, prayers, and [aartis](/wiki/Aarti "Aarti") are held daily at the temple between 02:30 and 23:00.[[160]](#cite_note-162)
* **The [Sankat Mochan Hanuman Temple](/wiki/Sankat_Mochan_Hanuman_Temple "Sankat Mochan Hanuman Temple")**, which is situated by the Asi River, is one of the sacred temples of the Hindu god [Hanuman](/wiki/Hanuman "Hanuman").[[161]](#cite_note-163) The present temple was built in the early 1900s by the educationist and [Indian independence figure](/wiki/Indian_independence_movement "Indian independence movement"), Pandit [Madan Mohan Malaviya](/wiki/Madan_Mohan_Malaviya "Madan Mohan Malaviya"), the founder of Banaras Hindu University.[[162]](#cite_note-ex-164) According to Hindu legend the temple was built on the spot where the medieval Hindu saint [Tulsidas](/wiki/Tulsidas "Tulsidas") had a vision of [Hanuman](/wiki/Hanuman "Hanuman").[[163]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECallewaert200090-165) During a 7 March 2006 terrorist attack, one of three explosions hit the temple while a wedding was in progress, and resulted in injuries to 30 people apart from 23 deaths.[[162]](#cite_note-ex-164) Following the attack, a permanent police post was installed inside the temple.[[164]](#cite_note-166)
* **There are two temples dedicated to the goddess Durga in Varanasi**: [Durga Mandir](/wiki/Durga_Mandir,_Varanasi "Durga Mandir, Varanasi") built in the 16th century (exact date not known), and Durga Kund (Sanskrit 'kund' meaning "pond or pool") built in the 18th century. A large number of Hindu devotees visit Durga Kund during [Navratri](/wiki/Navratri "Navratri") to worship the goddess [Durga](/wiki/Durga "Durga"). The temple, built in the [Nagara](/wiki/Nagara_architecture "Nagara architecture") architectural style, has multi-tiered spires[[159]](#cite_note-route-161) and is stained red with [ochre](/wiki/Ochre "Ochre"), representing the red colour of Durga. The building has a rectangular tank of water called the [Durga Kund](/wiki/Durga_Mandir,_Varanasi "Durga Mandir, Varanasi") ("Kund" meaning a pond or pool). During annual celebrations of [Nag Panchami](/wiki/Nag_Panchami "Nag Panchami"), the act of depicting the god Vishnu reclining on the serpent [Shesha](/wiki/Shesha "Shesha") is recreated in the Kund.[[165]](#cite_note-167) While the [Annapurna Temple](/wiki/Annapurna_Devi_Mandir "Annapurna Devi Mandir"), located nearby to the Kashi Vishwanath Temple, is dedicated to [Annapoorna devi](/wiki/Annapoorna_devi "Annapoorna devi"), the goddess of food,[[157]](#cite_note-temple-159) the Sankatha Temple adjacent to the Sindhia Ghat is dedicated to Sankatha, the goddess of remedy. The Sankatha Temple has a large sculpture of a lion and a cluster of nine smaller temples dedicated to the [nine planets](/wiki/Navagraha "Navagraha").[[157]](#cite_note-temple-159)
* **[Parshvanath Jain temple](/wiki/Parshvanath_Jain_temple,_Varanasi "Parshvanath Jain temple, Varanasi")** is the temple of Jain religion dedicated to Parshvanath, the 23rd Thirthankara who was born at Bhelpur in Varanasi. The idol deified in the temple is of black colour and 75 centimetres (30 inches) in height. It is located in Bhelapur about 5 kilometres (3.1 miles) from the centre of Varanasi city and 3 kilometres (1.9 miles) from the Benares Hindu University. It belongs to the Digambar sect of Jainism and is a holy tirtha or pilgrimage centre for Jains.
* Other temples of note are: the [Bharat Mata Mandir](/wiki/Bharat_Mata_Mandir "Bharat Mata Mandir"), dedicated to the national personification of India, which was inaugurated by [Mahatma Gandhi](/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi "Mahatma Gandhi") in 1936, the Kalabhairav Temple, the Mrithyunjay Mahadev Temple, and the [New Vishwanath Temple](/wiki/New_Vishwanath_Temple "New Vishwanath Temple") located in the campus of [BHU](/wiki/Banaras_Hindu_University "Banaras Hindu University"), the [Tulsi Manas Mandir](/wiki/Tulsi_Manas_Mandir "Tulsi Manas Mandir").[[157]](#cite_note-temple-159)


### Mosques


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Alamgir_Mosque_by_the_Ganges_ghats%2C_Varanasi.jpg/220px-Alamgir_Mosque_by_the_Ganges_ghats%2C_Varanasi.jpg)](/wiki/File:Alamgir_Mosque_by_the_Ganges_ghats,_Varanasi.jpg) [Alamgiri Mosque](/wiki/Alamgir_Mosque,_Varanasi "Alamgir Mosque, Varanasi")
There are 15 mosques of significant historical value in Varanasi. Of particular note are the Abdul Razzaq, Alamgir, Bibi Razia, Chaukhambha, Dhai Nim Kangore, Fatman, Ganje Shahada, Gyanavapi, and Hazrat Sayyed Salar Masud Dargah. Many of these mosques were constructed from the components of the Hindu shrines which were destroyed under the auspices of subsequent Muslim invaders or rulers. The two such well known mosques are the [Gyanvapi Mosque](/wiki/Gyanvapi_Mosque "Gyanvapi Mosque") and the [Alamgir Mosque](/wiki/Alamgir_Mosque "Alamgir Mosque").[[166]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOut2010259-168)


The Gyanvapi Mosque was built by the Mughal emperor Aurangzeb in 1664 CE, after destroying a Hindu temple.[[167]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDesai200323-169) *Gyan Vapi* (Sanskrit: "the well of knowledge"), the name of the mosque, is derived from a well of the same name located within the precincts of the mosque.[[168]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitra2002[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidNviJaunGDJMCpgPA95_95]-170) The remains of an erstwhile temple can be seen in the foundation, the columns and at the rear part of the mosque.[[169]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBettsMcCulloch2013213-171) The façade of the mosque is modelled partially on the [Taj Mahal](/wiki/Taj_Mahal "Taj Mahal")'s entrance.[[170]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAsher1992278-172) The mosque is administered by the Anjuman Inthazamiya Masajid (AIM).[[171]](#cite_note-173)


The Alamgiri Mosque was built in the 17th century by Aurangzeb over the ruins of a Hindu temple.[[172]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrowtherRajWheeler1984-174) The Hindu temple that was destroyed was dedicated to [Vishnu](/wiki/Vishnu "Vishnu"), and had been built by Beni Madhur Rao Scindia, a Maratha chieftain. When emperor Aurangzeb had captured Banaras, he had ordered total destruction of all Hindu temples there. Aurangzeb then built a mosque over the ruins of this temple in 1669[[173]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDunlopSykesJackson2001135-175) and named it as Alamagir Mosque in the name of his own honorific title "Alamgir" which he had adopted after becoming the emperor of Mughal empire.[[174]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKumar200390-176)[[169]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBettsMcCulloch2013213-171) The mosque is located at a prominent site above the [Panchganga Ghat](/w/index.php?title=Panchganga_Ghat&action=edit&redlink=1 "Panchganga Ghat (page does not exist)"), which is a funerary ghat facing the Ganges.[[175]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHussain1999342-177) The mosque is architecturally a blend of Islamic and Hindu architecture, particularly because of the lower part of the walls of the mosque having been built fully with the remains of the Hindu temple.[[174]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKumar200390-176) The mosque has high domes and minarets.[[176]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShetty201473-178)[[169]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBettsMcCulloch2013213-171) Two of its minarets had been damaged; one minaret crashed killing a few people and the other minaret was officially brought down because of stability concerns.[[169]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBettsMcCulloch2013213-171) Non-Muslims are not allowed to enter the mosque.[[177]](#cite_note-179) The mosque has a security cordon of a police force.[[178]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVit-Suzan201411-180)



### Shri Guru Ravidass Janam Asthan


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/RavidasiVaranasi.JPG/220px-RavidasiVaranasi.JPG)](/wiki/File:RavidasiVaranasi.JPG) Memorial of [Sant Ravidas](/wiki/Sant_Ravidas "Sant Ravidas") at [Sant Ravidas Ghat](/wiki/Sant_Ravidas_Ghat "Sant Ravidas Ghat")
[Shri Guru Ravidass Janam Asthan](/wiki/Shri_Guru_Ravidass_Janam_Asthan "Shri Guru Ravidass Janam Asthan"), at [Sir Gobardhan](/wiki/Sir_Gobardhan "Sir Gobardhan") is the ultimate place of pilgrimage or religious headquarters for followers of the [Ravidassia religion](/wiki/Ravidassia_religion "Ravidassia religion").[[179]](#cite_note-outlookindia.com_2015-181) The foundation stone was laid on 14 June 1965 on *Ashad Sankranti* day at the birthplace of Ravidas. The temple was completed in 1994.[[180]](#cite_note-182)



### Sarnath


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Sarnath_Residential_Area.jpg/300px-Sarnath_Residential_Area.jpg)](/wiki/File:Sarnath_Residential_Area.jpg) Sarnath, the suburb of Varanasi
[Sarnath](/wiki/Sarnath "Sarnath") is located 10 kilometres north-east of Varanasi near the confluence of the Ganges and the [Varuna](/wiki/Varuna "Varuna") rivers in [Uttar Pradesh](/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh "Uttar Pradesh"), India. The deer park in Sarnath is where [Gautama Buddha](/wiki/Gautama_Buddha "Gautama Buddha") first taught the [Dharma](/wiki/Dharma "Dharma"), and where the Buddhist [Sangha](/wiki/Sangha "Sangha") came into existence through the [enlightenment](/wiki/Enlightenment_in_Buddhism "Enlightenment in Buddhism") of [Kondanna](/wiki/Kondanna "Kondanna").[[181]](#cite_note-bn-183)


The city is mentioned by the Buddha as one of the four places of [pilgrimage](/wiki/Pilgrimage "Pilgrimage") to which his devout followers should visit. It was also the site of the Buddha's [Dhammacakkappavattana Sutta](/wiki/Dhammacakkappavattana_Sutta "Dhammacakkappavattana Sutta"), which was his first teaching after attaining enlightenment, in which he taught the [Four Noble Truths](/wiki/Four_Noble_Truths "Four Noble Truths") and the teachings associated with it.[[181]](#cite_note-bn-183)



Culture
-------


### Literature


See also: [The City of Varanasi in Literature](/wiki/The_City_of_Varanasi_in_Literature "The City of Varanasi in Literature")
Renowned Indian writers who have resided in the city were Kabir, Ravidas, and Tulsidas, who wrote much of his *Ram Charit Manas* here. Kulluka Bhatt wrote the best known account of *[Manusmriti](/wiki/Manusmriti "Manusmriti")* in Varanasi in the 15th century.[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*] Later writers of the city have included [Acharya Shukla](/wiki/Ramchandra_Shukla "Ramchandra Shukla"), [Baldev Upadhyaya](/wiki/Baldev_Upadhyaya "Baldev Upadhyaya"), [Bharatendu Harishchandra](/wiki/Bharatendu_Harishchandra "Bharatendu Harishchandra"), [Devaki Nandan Khatri](/wiki/Devaki_Nandan_Khatri "Devaki Nandan Khatri"), Premchand, [Hazari Prasad Dwivedi](/wiki/Hazari_Prasad_Dwivedi "Hazari Prasad Dwivedi"), [Jaishankar Prasad](/wiki/Jaishankar_Prasad "Jaishankar Prasad"), [Kshetresa Chandra Chattopadhyaya](/wiki/Kshetresa_Chandra_Chattopadhyaya "Kshetresa Chandra Chattopadhyaya"), Sudama Pandey (Dhoomil), [Vagish Shastri](/wiki/Vagish_Shastri "Vagish Shastri"), and [Vidya Niwas Mishra](/wiki/Vidya_Niwas_Mishra "Vidya Niwas Mishra").[*[citation needed](/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")*]


Several newspapers and journals are or were published in Varanasi such as *Varanasi Chandroday* and its successor *Kashivartaprakashika*, which became a weekly journal, first published on 1 June 1851.[[182]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMedhasananda2002653-184) The main newspaper is *[Aj](/wiki/Aj_(newspaper) "Aj (newspaper)")*, a Hindi-language nationalist newspaper first published in 1920.[[183]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKasbekar2006126-185) The newspaper was the bulwark of the [Indian National Congress](/wiki/Indian_National_Congress "Indian National Congress") and is a major newspaper of Hindi northern India.[[183]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKasbekar2006126-185)



### Art


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Wall_paintings%2C_Varanasi%2C_1973.jpg/220px-Wall_paintings%2C_Varanasi%2C_1973.jpg)](/wiki/File:Wall_paintings,_Varanasi,_1973.jpg) [Wall paintings](/wiki/Wall_painting "Wall painting"), Varanasi, 1973
Varanasi is a major centre of arts and designs. It is a producer of silks and brocades with gold and silver thread work, carpet weaving, wooden toys, bangles made of glass, ivory work, perfumes, artistic brass and copper ware and a variety of handicrafts.[[184]](#cite_note-topic-186)[[185]](#cite_note-bhu-187) The cantonment graveyard of the British Raj is now the location of Varanasi's Arts and Crafts.[[186]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETiwari20109-188)


Notable artists (musicians and dancers) and historians who are connected with the city include Thakur Jaidev Singh, Mahadev Prasad Mishra, [Bismillah Khan](/wiki/Bismillah_Khan "Bismillah Khan"), [Ravi Shankar](/wiki/Ravi_Shankar "Ravi Shankar"), [Girija Devi](/wiki/Girija_Devi "Girija Devi"), [Gopal Shankar Misra](/wiki/Gopal_Shankar_Misra "Gopal Shankar Misra"), [Gopi Krishna](/wiki/Gopi_Krishna_(dancer) "Gopi Krishna (dancer)"), [Kishan Maharaj](/wiki/Kishan_Maharaj "Kishan Maharaj"), [Lalmani Misra](/wiki/Lalmani_Misra "Lalmani Misra"), Premlata Sharma, [N. Rajam](/wiki/N._Rajam "N. Rajam"), [Siddheshwari Devi](/wiki/Siddheshwari_Devi "Siddheshwari Devi"), [Samta Prasad](/wiki/Samta_Prasad "Samta Prasad"), [Sitara Devi](/wiki/Sitara_Devi "Sitara Devi"),[[187]](#cite_note-189) Chhannulal Mishra, Rajan Sajan Mishra, Ritwik Sanyal, Soma Ghosh, Devashish Dey, Ramkrishna Das and Harish Tiwari.



### Music


Main article: [Music in Varanasi](/wiki/Music_in_Varanasi "Music in Varanasi")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Goswami_Tulsidas_Awadhi_Hindi_Poet.jpg/170px-Goswami_Tulsidas_Awadhi_Hindi_Poet.jpg)](/wiki/File:Goswami_Tulsidas_Awadhi_Hindi_Poet.jpg) Sant Goswami Tulsidas Awadhi Hindi poet and propagator of Bhakthi music in Varanasi
Varanasi's music tradition is traced to the [Pauranic](/wiki/Puranas "Puranas") days. According to ancient legend, Shiva is credited with evolving music and dance forms. During the medieval era, [Vaishnavism](/wiki/Vaishnavism "Vaishnavism"), a [Bhakti movement](/wiki/Bhakti_movement "Bhakti movement"), grew in popularity, and Varanasi became a thriving centre for musicians such as [Surdas](/wiki/Surdas "Surdas"), [Kabir](/wiki/Kabir "Kabir"), [Ravidas](/wiki/Ravidas "Ravidas"), [Meera](/wiki/Meera "Meera") and Tulsidas. During the monarchic rule of Govind Chandra in the 16th century, the [Dhrupad](/wiki/Dhrupad "Dhrupad") style of singing received royal patronage and led to other related forms of music such as Dhamar, Hori, and Chaturang. Presently the Dhrupad maestro Pandit [Ritwik Sanyal](/wiki/Ritwik_Sanyal "Ritwik Sanyal") from Varanasi is working for the revival of this art-music.[[188]](#cite_note-Music-190)


In recent times, Girija Devi, the native famous classical singer of [thumris](/wiki/Thumri "Thumri"), was widely appreciated and respected for her musical renderings.[[189]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroughtonEllingham200091-191) Varanasi is also associated with many great instrumentalists such as [Bismillah Khan](/wiki/Bismillah_Khan "Bismillah Khan")[[188]](#cite_note-Music-190) and [Ravi Shankar](/wiki/Ravi_Shankar "Ravi Shankar"), the famous [sitar](/wiki/Sitar "Sitar") player and musicologist who was given the highest civilian award of the country, the [Bharat Ratna](/wiki/Bharat_Ratna "Bharat Ratna").[[190]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruynBainAllardice2010470-192) Varanasi has joined the global bandwagon of UNESCO "Cities of Music" under the Creative Cities Network.[[191]](#cite_note-193)



### Festivals


On [Maha Shivaratri](/wiki/Maha_Shivaratri "Maha Shivaratri") (February), a procession of Shiva proceeds from the Mahamrityunjaya Temple to the Kashi Vishwanath Temple.[[65]](#cite_note-Varun-67) [Dhrupad Mela](/wiki/Dhrupad_Mela "Dhrupad Mela") is a five-day musical festival devoted to [dhrupad](/wiki/Dhrupad "Dhrupad") style held at Tulsi Ghat in February–March.[[192]](#cite_note-festival1-194) The [Sankat Mochan Hanuman Temple](/wiki/Sankat_Mochan_Hanuman_Temple "Sankat Mochan Hanuman Temple") celebrates [Hanuman Jayanti](/wiki/Hanuman_Jayanti "Hanuman Jayanti") (March–April), the birthday of Hanuman. A special [puja](/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism) "Puja (Hinduism)"), [aarti](/wiki/Aarti "Aarti"), and a public procession is organised.[[193]](#cite_note-sm-195)[[194]](#cite_note-196) Since 1923, the temple has organised a five-day classical music and dance concert festival named *[Sankat Mochan Sangeet Samaroh](/wiki/Sankat_Mochan_Sangeet_Samaroh "Sankat Mochan Sangeet Samaroh")*, wherein iconic artists from all parts of India are invited to perform.[[65]](#cite_note-Varun-67)


The [Ramlila](/wiki/Ramlila "Ramlila") of [Ramnagar](/wiki/Ramnagar,_Varanasi "Ramnagar, Varanasi") is a dramatic enactment of Rama's legend, as told in *Ramacharitamanasa*.[[63]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitra2002216-65) The plays, sponsored by Kashi Naresh, are performed in Ramnagar every evening for 31 days.[[63]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitra2002216-65) On the last day, the festivities reach a crescendo as Rama vanquishes the demon king [Ravana](/wiki/Ravana "Ravana").[[63]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitra2002216-65) Kashi Naresh [Udit Narayan Singh](/wiki/Udit_Narayan_Singh "Udit Narayan Singh") started this tradition around 1830.[[63]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitra2002216-65)


[Chhath Puja](/wiki/Chhath "Chhath") is celebrated on the sixth day of the lunar month of [Kartika](/wiki/K%C4%81rtika_(month) "Kārtika (month)") (October–November).[[195]](#cite_note-197)[[196]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESingh2009221-198)[[197]](#cite_note-199) The rituals are observed over four days.[[198]](#cite_note-200) They include holy bathing, fasting and abstaining from drinking water (vrata), standing in water, and offering prasad (prayer offerings) and arghya to the setting and rising sun.[[199]](#cite_note-201) Some devotees also perform a prostration march as they head for the river banks. Chhath puja is dedicated to the sun god "[Surya](/wiki/Surya "Surya")" and his sister "[Chhathi Maiya](/wiki/Chhath "Chhath")".[[200]](#cite_note-202) Chhath is considered as Mahaparva by the [Bhojpuri people](/wiki/Bhojpuri_people "Bhojpuri people").[[201]](#cite_note-203) It is said that the Chhath Mahaparva was started in Varanasi.[[202]](#cite_note-204)



[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nag_Nathaiya_festival_in_Varanasi.jpg/220px-Nag_Nathaiya_festival_in_Varanasi.jpg)](/wiki/File:Nag_Nathaiya_festival_in_Varanasi.jpg) [Krishna](/wiki/Krishna "Krishna") standing on serpent [Kaliya](/wiki/Kaliya "Kaliya") during [Nag Nathaiya](/wiki/Nag_Nathaiya_(Festival) "Nag Nathaiya (Festival)") festival in Varanasi
[Nag Nathaiya](/wiki/Nag_Nathaiya "Nag Nathaiya") is celebrated on the fourth lunar day of the dark fortnight of the Hindu month of [Kartik](/wiki/Kartik_(month) "Kartik (month)") (October–November). It commemorates the victory of [Krishna](/wiki/Krishna "Krishna") over the serpent [Kaliya](/wiki/Kaliya "Kaliya"). On this occasion, a large [Kadamba tree](/wiki/Neolamarckia_cadamba "Neolamarckia cadamba") (*Neolamarckia cadamba*) branch is planted on the banks of the Ganges so that a boy, playing the role of Krishna, can jump into the river on to the effigy representing Kaliya. He stands over the effigy in a dancing pose playing the [flute](/wiki/Flute "Flute"), while an audience watches from the banks of the river or from boats.[[203]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPintchman200569–70-205) *[Bharat Milap](/wiki/Bharat_Milap "Bharat Milap")* celebrates the meeting of Rama and his younger brother [Bharata](/wiki/Bharata_(Ramayana) "Bharata (Ramayana)") after the return of the former after 14 years of exile.[[65]](#cite_note-Varun-67) It is celebrated during October–November, a day after the festival of [Vijayadashami](/wiki/Vijayadashami "Vijayadashami"). Kashi Naresh attends this festival in his regal attire. The festival attracts a large number of devotees.[[204]](#cite_note-festival-206)


*Ganga Mahotsav* is a five-day music festival organised by the Uttar Pradesh Tourism Department, held in November–December. It culminates a day before [Kartik Purnima](/wiki/Kartik_Purnima "Kartik Purnima"), also called [the Ganges festival](/wiki/Dev_Deepawali_(Varanasi) "Dev Deepawali (Varanasi)"). On this occasion the Ganges is attended by thousands of pilgrims, release lighted lamps to float in the river from the ghats.[[65]](#cite_note-Varun-67)[[192]](#cite_note-festival1-194)


The primary Muslim festivals celebrated annually in the city are the ld-ul-fitr' ([Ramzan](/wiki/Ramadan "Ramadan")), [Bakrid](/wiki/Eid_al-Adha "Eid al-Adha"), [Mid-Sha'ban](/wiki/Mid-Sha%27ban "Mid-Sha'ban"), Bara Wafat and [Muharram](/wiki/Muharram "Muharram"). Additional festivals include Alvida and [Chehlum](/wiki/Arba%27een "Arba'een"). A non-religious festival observed by Muslims is Ghazi-miyan-ka-byaha ("the marriage of Ghazi Miyan").[[205]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESukul1974262-207)[[206]](#cite_note-208)



Education
---------


Main article: [Educational institutions in Varanasi](/wiki/List_of_educational_institutions_in_Varanasi "List of educational institutions in Varanasi")
[![The Banaras Hindu University in Varanasi](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/BHU_Main_Gate%2C_Banaras_Hindu_University_enhanced.jpg/220px-BHU_Main_Gate%2C_Banaras_Hindu_University_enhanced.jpg)](/wiki/File:BHU_Main_Gate,_Banaras_Hindu_University_enhanced.jpg) The Banaras Hindu University in Varanasi
Historically, Varanasi has been a centre for education in India, attracting students and scholars from across the country.[[207]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTESharma1995191-209)[[208]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGupta200641-210) Varanasi has an overall literacy rate of 80% (male literacy: 85%, female literacy: 75%).[[84]](#cite_note-uastats-86) It is home to a [number of colleges and universities](/wiki/List_of_educational_institutions_in_Varanasi "List of educational institutions in Varanasi"). Most notably, it is the site of [Banaras Hindu University](/wiki/Banaras_Hindu_University "Banaras Hindu University") (BHU), which is one of the largest residential universities in Asia with over 20,000 students.[[209]](#cite_note-211) The [Indian Institute of Technology (BHU) Varanasi](/wiki/Indian_Institute_of_Technology_(BHU)_Varanasi "Indian Institute of Technology (BHU) Varanasi") is designated an [Institute of National Importance](/wiki/List_of_Institutes_of_National_Importance "List of Institutes of National Importance") and is one of 16 [Indian Institutes of Technology](/wiki/Indian_Institutes_of_Technology "Indian Institutes of Technology"). Other colleges and universities in Varanasi include [Jamia-e-Imania](/wiki/Jamia-e-Imania "Jamia-e-Imania"), the Institute of Integrated Management and Technology, [Mahatma Gandhi Kashi Vidyapith](/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi_Kashi_Vidyapith "Mahatma Gandhi Kashi Vidyapith"), [Nav Sadhana Kala Kendra](/wiki/Nav_Sadhana_Kala_Kendra "Nav Sadhana Kala Kendra"), [Sampurnanand Sanskrit University](/wiki/Sampurnanand_Sanskrit_University "Sampurnanand Sanskrit University") and [Sri Agrasen Kanya P.G. College](/wiki/Sri_Agrasen_Kanya_P.G._College "Sri Agrasen Kanya P.G. College"). Various engineering colleges have been established in the outskirts of the city. Other notable universities and colleges include [Institute of Medical Sciences](/wiki/Institute_of_Medical_Sciences,_Banaras_Hindu_University "Institute of Medical Sciences, Banaras Hindu University"), [Sampurnanand Sanskrit Vishwavidyalaya](/wiki/Sampurnanand_Sanskrit_Vishwavidyalaya "Sampurnanand Sanskrit Vishwavidyalaya"), [Central Institute of Higher Tibetan Studies](/wiki/Central_Institute_of_Higher_Tibetan_Studies "Central Institute of Higher Tibetan Studies"), and [Harish Chandra Postgraduate College](/wiki/Harish_Chandra_Postgraduate_College "Harish Chandra Postgraduate College"). Some research oriented institutes were also established by the government such as International Rice Research Institute (IRRI),[[210]](#cite_note-212) [Indian Institute of Vegetable Research](/wiki/Indian_Council_of_Agricultural_Research "Indian Council of Agricultural Research")[[211]](#cite_note-213) and [National Seed Research and Training Centre](/wiki/Indian_Council_of_Agricultural_Research "Indian Council of Agricultural Research").[[212]](#cite_note-214)



[![Indian Institute of Technology in Varanasi](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Dept_of_Electrical_Engineering_IIT-BHU.jpg/220px-Dept_of_Electrical_Engineering_IIT-BHU.jpg)](/wiki/File:Dept_of_Electrical_Engineering_IIT-BHU.jpg) [Indian Institute of Technology](/wiki/Indian_Institute_of_Technology_(BHU)_Varanasi "Indian Institute of Technology (BHU) Varanasi") in Varanasi
Varanasi also has three [Kendriya Vidyalaya](/wiki/Kendriya_Vidyalaya "Kendriya Vidyalaya"). Among them [Kendriya Vidyalaya BHU](/wiki/Kendriya_Vidyalaya_BHU "Kendriya Vidyalaya BHU") holds the regional office of Varanasi Region of KVS and is seat of Deputy Commissioner. Kendriya Vidyalaya BHU is also accredited by the British Council. Other KVs are Kendriya Vidyalaya 39 GTC and Kendriya Vidyalaya DLW.


[St. Joseph's Convent School](/wiki/St._Joseph%27s_Convent_School,_Varanasi "St. Joseph's Convent School, Varanasi"), in Shivpur, Varanasi, was established by the Sisters of Our Lady of Providence of France as a Catholic (Christian) minority institution with the approval of the [Government of Uttar Pradesh](/wiki/Government_of_Uttar_Pradesh "Government of Uttar Pradesh"). It is an autonomous organisation under the diocese of the Bishop of Varanasi. It provides education not only to the Catholic Christian children, but also to others who abide by its rules.[[213]](#cite_note-215)


Another important institution is the [Central Hindu School](/wiki/Central_Hindu_School "Central Hindu School") in Kamachha. This was established by [Annie Besant](/wiki/Annie_Besant "Annie Besant") in July 1898 with the objective of imparting secular education. It is affiliated to the [Central Board of Secondary Education](/wiki/Central_Board_of_Secondary_Education "Central Board of Secondary Education") and is open to students of all cultures.[[214]](#cite_note-216)[[215]](#cite_note-217)


Schools in Varanasi are affiliated with the [Indian Certificate of Secondary Education](/wiki/Indian_Certificate_of_Secondary_Education "Indian Certificate of Secondary Education") (ICSE), the CBSE, or the [Uttar Pradesh Board of Technical Education](/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh_Board_of_Technical_Education "Uttar Pradesh Board of Technical Education") (U.P Board). The overall "state of education in Varanasi is ... not good."[[216]](#cite_note-sc-218) Schools in Varanasi vary widely in quality, with private schools outperforming government schools.[[216]](#cite_note-sc-218) In government schools, many teachers fail to come to class or to teach children.[[216]](#cite_note-sc-218) Some government schools lack basic equipment, such as blackboards and sufficient desks and chairs for all students.[[216]](#cite_note-sc-218) Private schools vary in quality, with the most expensive conducting lessons in English (seen as a key to children's success) and having computers in classrooms.[[216]](#cite_note-sc-218) Pupils attending the more expensive private schools, tended to come from upper-class families.[[216]](#cite_note-sc-218) Lower-cost private schools attracted children from lower-income families or those lower-income families with higher education aspirations.[[216]](#cite_note-sc-218) Government schools tend to serve lower-class children with lower education aspirations.[[216]](#cite_note-sc-218)



Media
-----


Main article: [Films shot in Varanasi](/wiki/List_of_films_shot_in_Varanasi "List of films shot in Varanasi")
Varanasi caters a lot of [shooting from different film industries](/wiki/List_of_films_shot_in_Varanasi "List of films shot in Varanasi") in India.[[217]](#cite_note-219) The temple town has emerged as a hub to [Hindi film industry](/wiki/Hindi_film_industry "Hindi film industry") and [South film industry](/wiki/Cinema_of_South_India "Cinema of South India").[[218]](#cite_note-220) Also, a chunk of Bhojpuri movies are shot in the city.[[219]](#cite_note-221) A few Bollywood movies that were shot, include [Gangs of Wasseypur](/wiki/Gangs_of_Wasseypur_%E2%80%93_Part_1 "Gangs of Wasseypur – Part 1"), [Raanjhanaa](/wiki/Raanjhanaa "Raanjhanaa"), [Piku](/wiki/Piku "Piku"), [Shubh Mangal Zyada Saavdhan](/wiki/Shubh_Mangal_Zyada_Saavdhan "Shubh Mangal Zyada Saavdhan") and [Super 30](/wiki/Super_30 "Super 30").[[220]](#cite_note-222)[[221]](#cite_note-223)[[222]](#cite_note-224)[[223]](#cite_note-225)[[224]](#cite_note-226) Some parts of the Hollywood movie [The Curious Case of Benjamin Button](/wiki/The_Curious_Case_of_Benjamin_Button_(film) "The Curious Case of Benjamin Button (film)") were also shot.[[225]](#cite_note-227) Web series such as [Mirzapur](/wiki/Mirzapur_(TV_series) "Mirzapur (TV series)") and [Asur](/wiki/Asur_(web_series) "Asur (web series)") were also shot in temple town.[[226]](#cite_note-228)[[227]](#cite_note-229)


Newspapers are widely available in Hindi and English. [Aj](/wiki/Aj_(newspaper) "Aj (newspaper)"), Hindi newspaper was established in 1920 in Varanasi.[[228]](#cite_note-About_us-230) Some publishers in the city are:



* Dainik Jagran[[229]](#cite_note-231)
* Hindustan[[230]](#cite_note-232)
* Amar Ujala[[231]](#cite_note-233)
* Jansandesh Times[[232]](#cite_note-234)
* [Rajasthan Patrika](/wiki/Rajasthan_Patrika "Rajasthan Patrika")[[233]](#cite_note-235)
* [Aj](/wiki/Aj_(newspaper) "Aj (newspaper)")[[228]](#cite_note-About_us-230)
* Times of India[[234]](#cite_note-236)
* Hindustan Times[[235]](#cite_note-237)


The city also hosts a Doordarshan Kendra, which was established in 1984 by the Hon'ble Prime Minister of India late Smt. Indira Gandhi. In 1998, Doordarshan studio was setup.[[236]](#cite_note-238)


FM/AM Stations available in the city are:[[237]](#cite_note-239)[[238]](#cite_note-240)[[239]](#cite_note-241)



* [Radio City](/wiki/Radio_City_(Indian_radio_station) "Radio City (Indian radio station)") 91.9 MHz
* [Red FM](/wiki/Red_FM_93.5 "Red FM 93.5") 93.5 MHz
* [BIG FM](/wiki/BIG_FM_92.7 "BIG FM 92.7") 95.0 MHz
* [Radio Mirchi](/wiki/Radio_Mirchi "Radio Mirchi") 98.3 MHz
* Radio Sunbeam 90.4 MHz
* [AIR Vividh Bharati](/wiki/Vividh_Bharati "Vividh Bharati") 100.6 MHz
* [Gyan Vani](/wiki/Gyan_Vani "Gyan Vani") 105.6 MHz
* AIR Varanasi 1242 AM


Mobile apps such as "InVaranasi", "Varanasi" and "LiveVNS" provide a wide range of information related to travel and local news.[[240]](#cite_note-242)[[241]](#cite_note-243)[[242]](#cite_note-244)



Sport
-----


[Basketball](/wiki/Basketball "Basketball"), [cricket](/wiki/Cricket "Cricket"), and [field hockey](/wiki/Field_hockey "Field hockey") are popular sports in Varanasi.[[243]](#cite_note-245) The main stadium in the city is the [Dr Sampurnanda Stadium](/wiki/Dr_Sampurnanda_Stadium "Dr Sampurnanda Stadium") (Sigra Stadium), where first-class cricket matches are held.[[244]](#cite_note-246) The city also caters an AstroTurf hockey stadium named, Dr. Bheemrao Ambedker National Hockey Stadium.[[245]](#cite_note-247)


The Department of Physical Education, [Faculty of Arts](/wiki/Faculty_of_Arts,_Banaras_Hindu_University "Faculty of Arts, Banaras Hindu University") of [BHU](/wiki/Banaras_Hindu_University "Banaras Hindu University") offers diploma courses in Sports Management, Sports Physiotherapy, Sports Psychology and Sports Journalism.[[246]](#cite_note-248) Also, BHU caters sports complexes including badminton court, tennis court, swimming pool and [amphitheater](/wiki/Amphitheater "Amphitheater").[[247]](#cite_note-249)


[Gymnastics](/wiki/Gymnastics "Gymnastics") is also popular in Varanasi, and many Indian girls practise outdoors at the ghats in the mornings which hosts [akhadas](/wiki/Pehlwani "Pehlwani"), where "morning exercise, a dip in the Ganges and a visit to Lord [Hanuman](/wiki/Hanuman "Hanuman")" forms a daily ritual.[[248]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTETiwari201047-250) Despite concerns regarding water quality, two swimming clubs offer swimming lessons in the Ganges.[[249]](#cite_note-251)


The Varanasi District Chess Sports Association (VDCSA) is based in Varanasi, affiliated to the regional Uttar Pradesh Chess Sports Association (UPCSA).[[250]](#cite_note-252)



Transport
---------


Varanasi is well-connected by air, rail, and road. One of the major factors in Varanasi, is its access to all parts of the country. Within the city mobility is provided by taxis, rickshaws, cycle rickshaws, and three-wheelers, but with certain restrictions in the old town area of the city.[[251]](#cite_note-reach-253)



### Air transport


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Vns_.jpg/220px-Vns_.jpg)](/wiki/File:Vns_.jpg) The [Lal Bahadur Shastri International Airport](/wiki/Lal_Bahadur_Shastri_International_Airport "Lal Bahadur Shastri International Airport") is the main airport that serves Varanasi.
Varanasi is served by [Lal Bahadur Shastri International Airport](/wiki/Lal_Bahadur_Shastri_International_Airport "Lal Bahadur Shastri International Airport") ([IATA](/wiki/IATA_airport_code "IATA airport code"): **VNS**, [ICAO](/wiki/ICAO_airport_code "ICAO airport code"): **VEBN**), which is approximately 26 km (16 mi) from the city centre in Babatpur.[[252]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMitra2002195-254) The airport's new terminal was inaugurated in 2010, and it was granted international airport status on 4 October 2012.[[253]](#cite_note-255) [Air India](/wiki/Air_India "Air India"), [Air India Express](/wiki/Air_India_Express "Air India Express"), [Buddha Air](/wiki/Buddha_Air "Buddha Air"), [IndiGo](/wiki/IndiGo "IndiGo"), [Malindo Air](/wiki/Malindo_Air "Malindo Air"), [SpiceJet](/wiki/SpiceJet "SpiceJet"), [SriLankan Airlines](/wiki/SriLankan_Airlines "SriLankan Airlines"), [Thai AirAsia](/wiki/Thai_AirAsia "Thai AirAsia"), [Thai Smile](/wiki/Thai_Smile "Thai Smile") and [Vistara](/wiki/Vistara "Vistara") operate flights from Varanasi to [Ahmedabad](/wiki/Ahmedabad "Ahmedabad"), [Bangkok](/wiki/Bangkok "Bangkok"), [Colombo](/wiki/Colombo "Colombo"), [Delhi](/wiki/Delhi "Delhi"), [Gaya](/wiki/Gaya,_India "Gaya, India"), [Kathmandu](/wiki/Kathmandu "Kathmandu"), [Khajuraho](/wiki/Khajuraho "Khajuraho"), [Sharjah](/wiki/Sharjah_(emirate) "Sharjah (emirate)"), [Kuala Lumpur](/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur "Kuala Lumpur"), [Mumbai](/wiki/Mumbai "Mumbai"), [Hyderabad](/wiki/Hyderabad,_India "Hyderabad, India"), [Bengaluru](/wiki/Bengaluru "Bengaluru"), [Goa](/wiki/Goa "Goa"), [Guwahati](/wiki/Guwahati "Guwahati"), [Jaipur](/wiki/Jaipur "Jaipur"), [Kolkata](/wiki/Kolkata "Kolkata") and several other cities.[[254]](#cite_note-256)


Over 3,010,702 passengers passed through the airport in 2019–20, making it the [20th busiest airport in India](/wiki/List_of_busiest_airports_in_India#Passenger_traffic "List of busiest airports in India"). The total aircraft movement for the session 2019–20 was 24,056 while cargo tonnage equalled 3,580. Total footfall of the international passengers for the session 2019–20 was 231,730.[[255]](#cite_note-257)[[256]](#cite_note-258)[[257]](#cite_note-259)



### Railways


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Varanasi_Railway_Station.JPG/220px-Varanasi_Railway_Station.JPG)](/wiki/File:Varanasi_Railway_Station.JPG) [Varanasi Junction](/wiki/Varanasi_Junction "Varanasi Junction"), is the main railway station which serves Varanasi.
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Manduadih_Railway_Station_at_night.jpg/220px-Manduadih_Railway_Station_at_night.jpg)](/wiki/File:Manduadih_Railway_Station_at_night.jpg) Banaras railway station at night
[Varanasi Junction](/wiki/Varanasi_Junction_railway_station "Varanasi Junction railway station"), commonly known as Varanasi Cantt Railway Station, is the city's largest railway station. More than 360,000 passengers and 240 trains pass through each day.[[258]](#cite_note-260) [Banaras railway station](/wiki/Banaras_railway_station "Banaras railway station") is also a Terminal station of Varanasi. Because of huge rush at Varanasi Junction the railway developed the station as a high facilitated terminal. [Varanasi City railway station](/wiki/Varanasi_City_railway_station "Varanasi City railway station") is also one of the railway stations in Varanasi district. It is 4 km North-East of Varanasi Junction railway station. It serves as Terminal station because of heavy rush at Varanasi Junction. [Mughalsarai Junction railway station](/wiki/Mughalsarai_Junction_railway_station "Mughalsarai Junction railway station") is also the important station in Varanasi suburban.


Some important express trains operating from the Varanasi Junction railway station and Manduadih railway station are: [Shiv Ganga Express](/wiki/Shiv_Ganga_Express "Shiv Ganga Express") runs between New Delhi Junction and Manduadih station while [Mahamana Express](/wiki/Mahamana_Express "Mahamana Express") runs between Varanasi junction and New Delhi Junction; the [Udhna Varanasi Express](/wiki/Udhna_Varanasi_Express "Udhna Varanasi Express") that runs between Udhna (Surat) junction and Varanasi, a distance of 1,398 kilometres (869 mi);[[259]](#cite_note-261) the [Kashi Vishwanath Express](/wiki/Kashi_Vishwanath_Express "Kashi Vishwanath Express") that runs between Varanasi and [New Delhi railway station](/wiki/New_Delhi_railway_station "New Delhi railway station");[[260]](#cite_note-262) the Kanpur Varanasi InterCity express, also called Varuna express, which runs over a distance of 355 kilometres (221 mi) and connects with [Lucknow](/wiki/Lucknow "Lucknow") (the capital city of Uttar Pradesh) and [Varanasi](/wiki/Kanpur "Kanpur");[[261]](#cite_note-263) and the [Sabarmati Express](/wiki/Sabarmati_Express "Sabarmati Express") which runs between Varanasi and Ahmedabad. [Vande Bharat Express](/wiki/Vande_Bharat_Express "Vande Bharat Express"), a [semi-high speed](/wiki/Higher-speed_rail "Higher-speed rail") train was launched in the month of February in 2019 in the Delhi-Varanasi route.[[262]](#cite_note-264) The train reduced the time travel between the two cities by 15 percent as compared to the [Shatabdi Express](/wiki/Shatabdi_Express "Shatabdi Express").[[263]](#cite_note-265)


Varanasi has following railway stations within the city suburbs:[[264]](#cite_note-266)[[265]](#cite_note-267)

### Roads


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Varanasi_Ring_Road_Phase_1.jpg/170px-Varanasi_Ring_Road_Phase_1.jpg)](/wiki/File:Varanasi_Ring_Road_Phase_1.jpg) Ring Road Phase I
[Auto rickshaws](/wiki/Auto_rickshaw "Auto rickshaw") and [E-rickshaws](/wiki/Electric_rickshaw "Electric rickshaw") are the most widely available forms of public transport in the old city.[[266]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruynBainAllardice2010467-268) In the outer regions of the city, taxis are available.[[266]](#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruynBainAllardice2010467-268) Daily commuters prefer city buses, which operate on specific routes of urban and suburban areas. The city buses are operated by Varanasi City Transport Service Limited.[[267]](#cite_note-269) Nearly, 120 buses are operated by Varanasi City Transport Service Limited.[[268]](#cite_note-270)


The following National Highways pass through Varanasi:[[269]](#cite_note-271)[[270]](#cite_note-272)[[271]](#cite_note-273)[[272]](#cite_note-274)[[273]](#cite_note-275)





| National Highway
 | Route
 | Total Length
 |
| --- | --- | --- |
| [NH 19](/wiki/National_Highway_19_(India) "National Highway 19 (India)") | [Delhi](/wiki/Delhi "Delhi") » [Mathura](/wiki/Mathura,_Uttar_Pradesh "Mathura, Uttar Pradesh") » [Agra](/wiki/Agra "Agra") » [Kanpur](/wiki/Kanpur "Kanpur") » [Allahabad](/wiki/Allahabad "Allahabad") » **Varanasi** » [Mohania](/wiki/Mohania "Mohania") » [Barhi](/wiki/Barhi,_Hazaribagh "Barhi, Hazaribagh") » [Palsit](/wiki/Palsit "Palsit") » [Kolkata](/wiki/Kolkata "Kolkata") | 1,323 km (822 mi)
 |
| [NH 233](/wiki/National_Highway_233_(India) "National Highway 233 (India)") | **Varanasi** » [Azamgarh](/wiki/Azamgarh "Azamgarh") » [Tanda](/wiki/Tanda,_Ambedkar_Nagar "Tanda, Ambedkar Nagar") » [Basti](/wiki/Basti,_Uttar_Pradesh "Basti, Uttar Pradesh") » [Siddharthnagar](/wiki/Siddharthnagar "Siddharthnagar") » [Lumbini](/wiki/Lumbini "Lumbini") ([Nepal](/wiki/Nepal "Nepal"))
 | 288 km (179 mi)
 |
| [NH 35](/wiki/National_Highway_35_(India) "National Highway 35 (India)") | [Mahoba](/wiki/Mahoba "Mahoba") » [Banda](/wiki/Banda,_Uttar_Pradesh "Banda, Uttar Pradesh") » [Chitrakoot](/wiki/Chitrakoot_district "Chitrakoot district") » [Allahabad](/wiki/Allahabad "Allahabad") » [Mirzapur](/wiki/Mirzapur "Mirzapur") » **Varanasi** | 346 km (215 mi)
 |
| [NH 31](/wiki/National_Highway_31_(India) "National Highway 31 (India)") | [Unnao](/wiki/Unnao "Unnao") » [Raebareli](/wiki/Raebareli "Raebareli") » [Pratapgarh](/wiki/Pratapgarh,_Uttar_Pradesh "Pratapgarh, Uttar Pradesh") » **Varanasi** » [Patna](/wiki/Patna "Patna") » [Samsi](/wiki/Samsi,_Malda "Samsi, Malda") | 968 km (601 mi)
 |
| NH 7
 | **Varanasi** » [Jabalpur](/wiki/Jabalpur "Jabalpur") » [Nagpur](/wiki/Nagpur "Nagpur") » [Hyderabad](/wiki/Hyderabad "Hyderabad") » [Bangalore](/wiki/Bangalore "Bangalore") » [Kanyakumari](/wiki/Kanyakumari "Kanyakumari") | 2,369 km (1,472 mi)
 |


The heavy traffic of the city is monitored through Integrated Traffic Management System. The smart traffic management system equips the city with automatic signal control system, separate signal system for pedestrians, traffic management centre at state level, area traffic control system, corridor management and dynamic traffic indicators for smooth movement of traffic.[[274]](#cite_note-276) Varanasi Traffic Police keeps an eye through Smart Command and Control Centre.[[275]](#cite_note-277)[[276]](#cite_note-278)



### Inland waterways


[National Waterway 1](/wiki/National_Waterway_1 "National Waterway 1") passes through Varanasi. In 2018, a new inland port was established on the banks of Ganges River.[[277]](#cite_note-279) The [Multi-Modal Terminal](/wiki/Varanasi_Multi-Modal_Terminal "Varanasi Multi-Modal Terminal") is designed to handle 1.26 million metric tons of cargo every year and covers an area of 34 hectares.[[278]](#cite_note-280) Nearly, ₹170 crore was invested by the Government to setup an inland port.[[279]](#cite_note-281) Maersk started its container service in 2019 by moving 16 containers on [NW-1](/wiki/National_Waterway_1 "National Waterway 1") from Varanasi to Kolkata. The port also catered PepsiCo, IFFCO Fertilizers, Emami Agrotech and Dabur for cargo movement.[[280]](#cite_note-282)



Projects
--------


Due to growing population and industrial demands, the city is being implanted with several infrastructural projects.[[281]](#cite_note-283) In fiscal year 2014–18, the city was awarded with projects worth ₹30,000 crore.[[282]](#cite_note-284) The city is being invested by both private and public players in different sectors.[[283]](#cite_note-285) Currently, there are many undergoing projects and many have been planned.



### Road


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Roads_in_Varanasi_Cantonment.JPG/220px-Roads_in_Varanasi_Cantonment.JPG)](/wiki/File:Roads_in_Varanasi_Cantonment.JPG) Road in Varanasi Cantonment
The Government is executing seven road projects connecting Varanasi, the total project cost being ₹7100 crores and the total length of the project being 524 km.
Some important projects are:



* Six lane Varanasi-Aurangabad section of NH-19[[284]](#cite_note-:4-286)
* Six lane Varanasi-Allahabad NH-19[[285]](#cite_note-287)
* Four lane Varanasi-Gorakhpur NH-29[[286]](#cite_note-288)
* Ghagra Bridge-Varanasi section of NH-233[[284]](#cite_note-:4-286)
* Four lane Varanasi-Azamgarh Section NH-233[[287]](#cite_note-289)
* Four lane Varanasi-Sultanpur NH-56[[288]](#cite_note-290)
* New four lane Varanasi-Ayodhya Highway[[289]](#cite_note-291)
* Varanasi Ring Road Phase – 2[[290]](#cite_note-292)
* Ganga Expressway Phase – 2[[291]](#cite_note-293)
* Varanasi-Ranchi-Kolkata Greenfield Expressway[[292]](#cite_note-294)
* Purvanchal Link Expressway[[293]](#cite_note-295)


### Accommodation


All ranges of accommodations are available. Hotels, lodges are available. Lots of dharamshalas or nattukottai chatrams are also available. For yatris who want to breath their last in this holy city, specific accommodations are also available.



### Railways


[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/GTY_based_WDG-4_locos_with_Freight_Tanker_at_Malkajgiri_01.JPG/220px-GTY_based_WDG-4_locos_with_Freight_Tanker_at_Malkajgiri_01.JPG)](/wiki/File:GTY_based_WDG-4_locos_with_Freight_Tanker_at_Malkajgiri_01.JPG) [BLW](/wiki/Banaras_Locomotive_Works "Banaras Locomotive Works") manufactured locomotives hauling load across the nation.
In 2018, the budget reflected undergoing rail projects of worth ₹4500 crore. Some important projects are:[[294]](#cite_note-296)



* 3rd rail line between Varanasi-Mughalsarai[[295]](#cite_note-297)
* New Delhi-Varanasi High Speed Rail Corridor[[296]](#cite_note-298)
* Eastern Dedicated Freight Corridor (Jeonathpur Railway Station)[[297]](#cite_note-299)
* [Kashi Railway Station](/wiki/Kashi_railway_station "Kashi railway station") to be developed as [Intermodal Station (IMS)](/wiki/Intermodal_passenger_transport "Intermodal passenger transport")[[298]](#cite_note-300)[[299]](#cite_note-301)


### Airport


* Extension of runway by 1325 meters (First of its kind: National Highway under the airport runway)[[300]](#cite_note-302)
* New terminal with passenger capacity of 4.5 million per year[[301]](#cite_note-303)


### Metro


The [Varanasi Metro](/wiki/Varanasi_Metro "Varanasi Metro") is a [rapid transit](/wiki/Rapid_transit "Rapid transit") proposed for Varanasi. The proposed system consists of two lines, spanning from [BHEL](/wiki/Bharat_Heavy_Electricals "Bharat Heavy Electricals") to [Banaras Hindu University](/wiki/Banaras_Hindu_University "Banaras Hindu University") (19.35 km) and Benia Bagh to [Sarnath](/wiki/Sarnath "Sarnath") (9.885 km). The feasibility study of the project was done by [RITES](/wiki/RITES "RITES") and was completed in June 2015. Metro Rail is likely to be completed around 2020. There will be 26 stations, including 20 underground and six elevated on the two lines, which includes total length of 29.235 km consisting of 23.467 km underground, while 5.768 km will be elevated.[[302]](#cite_note-304)[[303]](#cite_note-305)[[304]](#cite_note-306)[[305]](#cite_note-307)


The total estimated completion cost for construction of Varanasi Metro is estimated to be ₹13,133 crore.[[306]](#cite_note-308) The project is envisaged to be undertaken as a joint venture (JV) project between the [Government of India](/wiki/Government_of_India "Government of India") (GoI) and the [Government of Uttar Pradesh](/wiki/Government_of_Uttar_Pradesh "Government of Uttar Pradesh") (GoUP) with 50:50 equity partnerships. To maintain the financial viability of the project, additional grants have been proposed from the two governments in addition to their equity contribution.

(2012), ["Banaras"](https://books.google.com/books?id=B105DQAAQBAJ&pg=PA115), in Juergensmeyer, Mark; Roof, Wade Clark (eds.), *Encyclopedia of Global Religion, Volume 1*, pp. 114–116, [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9780761927297](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780761927297 "Special:BookSources/9780761927297"), Varanasi is the city's revived, post-independence designation, which combines the names of two rivers on either side of it.
15. **[^](#cite_ref-combined-central_16-0)** 

	* Fouberg, Erin H.; Moseley, William G. (2018), *Understanding World Geography*, New York: John Wiley & Sons, p. 173, [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9781119473169](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781119473169 "Special:BookSources/9781119473169"), [OCLC](/wiki/OCLC_(identifier) "OCLC (identifier)") [1066742384](//www.worldcat.org/oclc/1066742384), The city of Varanasi, India, is central to the death tradition in Hinduism. Hindus see Varanasi as the world of death and life, and some make pilgrimages to Varanasi to die. In Hindu tradition, if a person dies in the holy city of Varanasi on the Ganges River, he or she is attains moksha, or freedom from the cycle of death and rebirth. Pilgrims travel to Varanasi to cremate their deceased relatives on the ghats along the river.
	* [Eck, Diana](/wiki/Diana_Eck "Diana Eck") (2013) [1981], *Banaras, the City of Light*, Alfred Knopf Inc, [Columbia University Press], p. 324, –No other city on earth is as famous for death as is Banāras. More than for her temples and magnificent ghāts, more than for her silks and brocades, Banāras, the Great Cremation Ground, is known for death. At the center of the city along the riverfront is Manikarnikā, the sanctuary of death, with its ceaselessly smoking cremation pyres. The burning ghāt extends its influence and the sense of its presence throughout the city.
	* Parry, Jonathan P. (2000) [1994], [*Death in Banaras*](https://books.google.com/books?id=r10f4uwzcosC), Lewis Henry Morgan Lectures, Cambridge University Press, p. 1, [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9780521466257](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780521466257 "Special:BookSources/9780521466257"), As a place to die, to dispose of the physical remains of the deceased and to perform the rites which ensure that the departest attains a 'good state' after death, the north Indian city of Banaras attracts pilgrims and mourners from all over the Hindu world.
	* Singh, Ravi Nandan (2022). *Dead in Banaras: An Ethnography of Funeral Travelling*. Oxford, UK: Oxford University Press. The present-day Banaras, at first sign, is a new place. Rightly so, the baton must then pass on to an all new chronicling of the place. Yet, a connecting link, as always, may come into play, between the book's time and other times of Banaras. Let me give an example of what such a connection might look like. Jonathan Parry (1994) in his classic *Death in Banaras* laments in the preface to the book that he could not incorporate the coming in of the electric crematorium in his descriptions of the funerary organization in Banaras. Two decades later into my fieldwork, I found that it is in part, the efficiency of the open-air, manual cremation that Parry so effectively captures in his book that explains how a promising symbol of industrial modernity, the electric crematorium, falls short of the typecast. In the years between his book and my fieldwork, the electric crematorium sat lonely and was sparingly used against the cheer of the always-on, busy, manual pyres whose flames continue to dot the scene of the ghats in a contrasting relief. In this above sense, I believe, Parry already provides us a portrait of the electric crematorium's social imaginary in Banaras. The question of the shift from wooden pyres to the electric crematorium is then not about competing technologies but that of ethics with which the dead are tended to amidst the assemblies of funeral travellers.
16. **[^](#cite_ref-garces-foley-varanasi_17-0)** Garces-Foley, Kathleen (2022), "At the Intersection of Death and Religion", in Garces-Foley, Kathleen (ed.), *Death and Religion in a Changing World* (2 ed.), London and New York: Routledge, p. 186, [doi](/wiki/Doi_(identifier) "Doi (identifier)"):[10.4324/9781003126997](https://doi.org/10.4324%2F9781003126997), [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-0-367-64930-2](/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-367-64930-2 "Special:BookSources/978-0-367-64930-2"), It is not uncommon for immigrants to discover that their long-established death practices are deemed unacceptable by civil authorities in their new home. We see this for example in the experiences of Sikhs and Hindus living in Sweden and the United States where open cremation pyres are not permitted. Market forces and social context also shape religious practices by limiting access to some goods and services while promoting others and offering new possibilities for action. ... The logistical difficulty of transporting a body from the United States or the UK to the auspicious city of Varanasi, India, for cremation is surmounted by entrepreneurial service providers who manage the process for Hindu customers.
17. **[^](#cite_ref-arnold-benares_18-0)** Arnold, David (2021), *Burning the Dead: Hindu Nationhood and the Global Construction of Indian Tradition*, Oakland: University of California Press, p. 11, [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9780520379343](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780520379343 "Special:BookSources/9780520379343"), [LCCN](/wiki/LCCN_(identifier) "LCCN (identifier)") [2020026923](//lccn.loc.gov/2020026923), While Benares is undeniably central to the performance and perception of modern Indian cremation, that history cannot be told from Benares alone. Rather, ... the narrative needs to encompass colonial India’s two main metropolises, Bombay (Mumbai) and Calcutta (Kolkata), as well as the movement of Indians overseas and their memorialization abroad. ... The history of cremation in India is far more than the history of traditional rites and practices that it is conventionally taken to be—if tradition is assumed to mean “timeless” custom and immutable belief. On the contrary, cremation in modern India and across the South Asian diaspora is a history of contestation and change, of longing and denial, adaptation and innovation. India, too, has gifted to the world a modern cremation movement, though its meaning, form, and global resonance necessarily differed substantially from the Western cremation movement with which it was nearly contemporaneous.
18. **[^](#cite_ref-combined-muslim-weaver_20-0)** 

	* Williams, Philippa. "Working Narratives of Intercommunity Harmony in Varanasi's Silk Sari Industry". In Jeffrey, Roger; Jeffrey, Craig; Lerche, Jens (eds.). *Development Failure and Identity Politics in Uttar Pradesh*. SAGE. pp. 211–238. [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-81-321-1663-9](/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-81-321-1663-9 "Special:BookSources/978-81-321-1663-9"). 'Varanasi … is the city where Hindus and Muslims … are interwoven like threads as in the lovely silk saris for which Kashi (Varanasi) is so famous for (Puniyani, 2006).'(quoted) Varanasi is most often represented as a sacred Hindu pilgrimage centre (see Eck, 1983), as its social and cultural urban spaces have been often examined through the imagined and lived realities of Hinduism (Hertel and Humes, 1993; Parry, 1994; Singh and Rana, 2002). But it is also home to a sizeable Muslim population, which in 2001 comprised 30 per cent of the city’s residents, significantly more than the percentage of Muslims in UP (Census of India, 2001). Unlike the city’s majority Hindu inhabitants (63 per cent), who occupy a range of occupations in different economic sectors, Muslims in the city are predominantly involved in the production of silk fabrics, as well as other smaller artisanal industries (see Kumar, 1988). Muslims first settled in Varanasi in the eleventh century, when, following the defeat of an invading Muslim army, women, children and civilians were permitted to remain on the northern side of the city and serve the Hindu kings. Many learned the craft of weaving, incorporating their skills and designs into the fabrics. Jean-Baptiste Tavernier, the French explorer and cultural anthropologist, visited Varanasi between 1660 and 1665 and reported that in the courtyard of a rest house in the Chowk area the trading of *reshmi* (silk) and *suti* (cotton) fabrics was taking place between Muslim *karigars* (artisans or craftsmen) and Hindu Mahajans (traders)
	* Puniyani, Ram (21 April 2006), ["Tackling Terrorism – Varanasi, Jama Masjid Show the Way"](https://countercurrents.org/comm-puniyani210406.htm), *CounterCurrents.Org*, Varanasi, like many other cities of the country is the city where Hindus and Muslim of the city are interwoven like threads as in the lovely silk saris for which Kashi is so famous for. This town has hundreds and hundreds of Muslims artisans weaving the beautiful silk apparels, which are sold by the Hindu traders. This is also a city where on one hand we see the likes of Munshi Premchand, who wrote in Urdu as Nawab Rai and also crafted the acme of Hindi literature, which is not only progressive but is also a celebration of composite traditions of the country. This is also the city of the likes of Ustad Bismillah Khans, whose Shahanai begins with devotion to Hindu deities and hums the pleasant enchanting music into the ears of the whole nation. It is also the city which like most of the cities of the country, highlights the intercommunity amity in its most pleasant flavor.
	* Mallet, Victor (2017). *River of Life, River of Death: The Ganges and India's Future*. Oxford, UK: Oxford University Press. [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9780198786177](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780198786177 "Special:BookSources/9780198786177"). [LCCN](/wiki/LCCN_(identifier) "LCCN (identifier)") [2017939064](//lccn.loc.gov/2017939064). Modi, however, went out of his way to court Varanasi’s Muslims—they account for nearly a quarter of the city’s 1.6 million voters—and to emphasize its multicultural, syncretic traditions when he was on the campaign trail in 2014. He praised not only Hindu but also Muslim cultural figures, including the musician Bismillah Khan, and said Khan was arguably the greatest symbol of Ganga-Jamuni tehzeeb (Ganga-Yamuna culture), a riverine phrase often used to describe the intertwined Hindu-Muslim culture of north India where those two rivers flow. Modi also targeted the important Muslim community of sari weavers in the district of Lallapura. Varanasi’s silk wedding saris, lavishly designed and interwoven with gold thread, are much sought after by Indians from across the country and from overseas, and Modi promised to help the weavers acquire modern technology, quality raw materials and better marketing skills so they could compete with Chinese clothing manufacturers. ‘The weavers of Varanasi are an integral part of the city’s history', he wrote in a blog post on his nomination day. ‘It is my resolve to ensure that they stand on their own feet with pride and their future generations have a bright future.’ (pp. 51–52) An earlier great poet, son of Varanasi and symbol of north India’s syncretic traditions, was the fifteenth-century Kabir, whose pithy lines in Hindi are still much quoted today by Indians over social media. Again, the legends surrounding his life are confused. He may have been born into a low-caste Muslim community of weavers or been a Hindu by birth. But he famously mocked the priesthoods and the rituals of both Muslims and Hindus, even to the extent of deliberately leaving holy Varanasi to die in an obscure town, when most north Indians would be heading in the other direction and yearning to expire within the boundaries of the city to find salvation. His contempt for organized religion is reflected in the legend of his death: Hindu and Muslim devotees argued over who should claim the poet’s remains, but when the cloth covering his body was lifted, they found nothing underneath but a spray of flowers. (pp 60–61)
	* Kumar, Nita (2017) [1988], [*The Artisans of Banaras: Popular Culture and Identity, 1880–1986*](https://books.google.com/books?id=bZYrDgAAQBAJ), Princeton Legacy Library, Princeton University Press, p. 15, 18, 137, [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9781400886999](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781400886999 "Special:BookSources/9781400886999"), The simplicty of weavers' weddings is a contrast even to those of other Muslims, such as the Pathans. (p. 15) ... Contrary to the experience of most artisan production in modern times, the silk weaving industry has actually flourished, and remained the commercial backbone of the Hindu pilgrimage and religious centre of Banaras. (p. 18) The debate on how Muslims in other parts of South Asia adjust an "Islamic" identity with a territorial-cultural one which is heavily oriented towards local Hinduism is very instructive with regard to our material. The weaver of Banaras is as shaukeen a man as the Hindu and central to his life-style is the love of the outside, of akharas, and of music.(pp. 137–138)
	* San Chirico, Kerry P. C. (2012), ["Banaras"](https://books.google.com/books?id=B105DQAAQBAJ&pg=PA115), in Juergensmeyer, Mark; Roof, Wade Clark (eds.), *Encyclopedia of Global Religion, Volume 1*, pp. 114–116, [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9780761927297](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780761927297 "Special:BookSources/9780761927297"), This was the period in which an enduring cultural imprint was made in ... the establishment of *muhallas* or neighborhoods that exist to this day, in the presence of Sufi shrines dotting the landscape, and in the creation of a singular syncretic culture ... Today Islam accounts for more than one-third of Varanasi city's population. There are as many Muslims here as there are Brahmans, the majority of whom are weavers. The relationship between the Muslims who weave Banaras's famous saris and the Hindus who sell them explains in part by historically there has been less communal tension here than in other cities throughout South Asia.

In some specific cases the symbolism does seem specifically Buddhist. The capital at Sarnath, for example, has four wheels carved on its drum (see Figure 3.4). Critically, this is the only Mauryan capital that includes wheel motifs. It seems unlikely that it is merely coincidental that the capital was located at Sarnath—the location of the Buddha’s first sermon, the place where the Buddha first turned the wheel of Dharma. Rather, it seems very likely that the wheel motif, at least at Sarnath, symbolized the wheel of Dharma in the specifically Buddhist sense of the term

25. **[^](#cite_ref-metcalf-imperial-projects_27-0)** Metcalf, Barbara (2009). ["Introduction"](https://books.google.com/books?id=pR0LzVCpfw8C&pg=PA15). In Metcalf, Barbara D. 26. **[^](#cite_ref-bourke-burke-benares_28-0)** Bourke, Richard (8 September 2015), [*Empire and Revolution: The Political Life of Edmund Burke*](https://books.google.com/books?id=E6eeDwAAQBAJ&pg=PA843), Princeton University Press, p. 843, [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9780691145112](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780691145112 "Special:BookSources/9780691145112"), Since 1724 Awadh, “in extent about the size of England,” had enjoyed effective autonomy as a quasi-independent province within the Mughal Empire. It came to terms with the British after the Battle of Buxar in 1764, finally signing up to the Treaty of Benares in 1773. This imposed on the Wazir of Awadh, Shuja-ud-Daula, the obligation to accept troops stationed in his territory while paying a subsidy to the British for the privilege. The Wazir’s successor, Asaf-ud-Daula, agreed to increase this subsidy under the Treaty of Faizabad in 1775, at the same time ceding Benares to the Company.
27. **[^](#cite_ref-markovitz-benares_29-0)** Markovitz, Claude (24 September 2004), ["Birth of the British Empire in India (1765–1818)"](https://books.google.com/books?id=r2OKvG5wbaAC&pg=PA254), in Markovits, Claude (ed.), *A History of Modern India, 1480–1950*, Anthem Press, [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9781843311522](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781843311522 "Special:BookSources/9781843311522"), In the face of the hostility of all the dynamic forces of the country, the threat of the Marathas looming large on the frontiers, Asaf could count henceforth only on the help of the English, who exerted themselves to use this tumultuous situation to the full. In exchange for their military protection, the *nawab* granted them as early as 1775 the control of the region of Benares, in addition to a substantial increase in indemnity.
28. **[^](#cite_ref-bayly-benares-division_30-0)** [Bayly, Christopher](/wiki/Christopher_Bayly "Christopher Bayly") (1983), [*Rulers, townsmen, and bazaars: north Indian society in the age of British expansion, 1770–1870*](https://archive.org/details/rulerstownsmenba0000bayl/page/n15/mode/2up?), Cambridge University Press, pp. xii–xiii, [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [9780521229326](/wiki/Special:BookSources/9780521229326 "Special:BookSources/9780521229326"), In 1801 a large area of Awadh situated in the Doab and Rohilkhand were ceded to the British. It was added to districts conquered from the Marathas in 1803–4 around Delhi and Agra to form the 'Conquered and Ceded Provinces' of the British Bengal Presidency. The term 'Western Provinces' and later 'North-Western Provinces' came into gradual use to describe this area and the adjoining Benares Division; ... In 1856 the remaining 'Reserved Dominions' of the ruler of Awadh were annexed to become the British Province of Oudh under a Chief Commissioner. In 1901 the two provinces were amalgamated to become the United Province of Agra and Oudh.

Akbar then marched to Allahabad and onto Banaras which was sacked because it closes its gates against him
50. **[^](#cite_ref-52)** Rima Hooja (2006). [*A history of Rajasthan*](https://books.google.com/books?id=tosMAQAAMAAJ). pp. 493–495. [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [978-8129108906](/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-8129108906 "Special:BookSources/978-8129108906"). Among the architectural legacies left by Man Singh are the palaces within Amber fort, Man Mandir, Man Chat and Sarovar Ghat at Varanasi, the Govind Dev temple at Vrindaban, and temples at Pushkar, Manpur, Puri, etc. He also built forts at Salimpur (Bengal), Manihari (Bihar), Ramgarh (Dhoondhar), founded the towns of Akbarnagar (Rajmahal), Manpur (near Gaya), and the small township of Baikunthpur (now called Baikathpur, in Bihar’s Patna district), and carried out massive repairs and fresh construction, including of palaces, at the fort of Rohtas
